<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Four Policemen</h1>			<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
				<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>				<div id="contentSub"></div>
				<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>				<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
				<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cairo_conference.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Cairo_conference.jpg/220px-Cairo_conference.jpg" width="220" height="168" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Cairo_conference.jpg/330px-Cairo_conference.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Cairo_conference.jpg/440px-Cairo_conference.jpg 2x" data-file-width="580" data-file-height="442" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cairo_conference.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Chiang_Kai-shek" title="Chiang Kai-shek">Chiang Kai-shek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a> met at the <a href="/wiki/Cairo_Conference_(1943)" class="mw-redirect" title="Cairo Conference (1943)">Cairo Conference</a> in 1943 during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The term "<b>Four Policemen</b>" refers to a <a href="/wiki/Post-war" title="Post-war">post-war</a> council consisting of the Big Four that <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">U.S. President</a> <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a> proposed as a guarantor of <a href="/wiki/World_peace" title="World peace">world peace</a>. The members of the <b>Big Four</b>, called the <b>Four Powers</b> during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, were the four major <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">Allies of World War II</a>: the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> and <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_China_(1912%E2%80%931949)" title="Republic of China (1912–1949)">China</a>. The <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> envisioned by Roosevelt consisted of three branches: an executive branch comprising the Big Four, an enforcement branch composed of the same four <a href="/wiki/Great_power" title="Great power">great powers</a> acting as the Four Policemen or <b>Four Sheriffs</b>, and an international assembly representing the member nations of the UN.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoopesBrinkley1997100_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoopesBrinkley1997100-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Four Policemen would be responsible for keeping order within their spheres of influence: Britain in its <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">empire</a> and in Western Europe; the Soviet Union in Eastern Europe and the central Eurasian landmass; China in East Asia and the Western Pacific; and the United States in the Western hemisphere. As a preventive measure against new wars, countries other than the Four Policemen were to be disarmed. Only the Four Policemen would be allowed to possess any weapons more powerful than a rifle.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGaddis197225_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaddis197225-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As a compromise with internationalist critics, the Big Four nations became the <a href="/wiki/Permanent_members_of_the_United_Nations_Security_Council" title="Permanent members of the United Nations Security Council">permanent members</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council" title="United Nations Security Council">United Nations Security Council</a>, with significantly less power than envisioned in the Four Policemen proposal.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGaddis197227_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaddis197227-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> When the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> was officially established in later 1945, <a href="/wiki/Provisional_Government_of_the_French_Republic" title="Provisional Government of the French Republic">France</a> was in due course added as the fifth member of the council at that time<sup id="cite_ref-UN_year_book_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_year_book-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> due to the insistence of Churchill.
</p>
<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Four_Policemen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Four_Policemen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a></div>
<p>During <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, President Roosevelt initiated post-war plans for the creation of a new and more durable international organization that would replace the former <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a>. Prior to the war, Roosevelt had initially been a supporter of the League of Nations, but he lost confidence in the League due to its ineffectiveness at preventing the outbreak of the second World War. Roosevelt wanted to create an international organization that secured global peace through the unified efforts of the world's <a href="/wiki/Great_powers" class="mw-redirect" title="Great powers">great powers</a>, rather than through the <a href="/wiki/Wilsonianism" title="Wilsonianism">Wilsonian</a> notions of international consensus and collaboration that guided the League of Nations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGaddis197224_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaddis197224-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> By 1935, he told his foreign policy adviser <a href="/wiki/Sumner_Welles" title="Sumner Welles">Sumner Welles</a>: "The League of Nations has become nothing more than a debating society, and a poor one at that!"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWelles1951182–204_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWelles1951182–204-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Roosevelt criticized the League of Nations for representing the interests of too many nations. The President said to the Soviet foreign minister <a href="/wiki/Vyacheslav_Molotov" title="Vyacheslav Molotov">Vyacheslav Molotov</a> that "he could not visualize another League of Nations with 100 different signatories; there were simply too many nations to satisfy, hence it was a failure and would be a failure".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUnited_States_Department_of_State1942573_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUnited_States_Department_of_State1942573-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Roosevelt's proposal in 1941 was to create a new international body led by a "trusteeship" of great powers that would oversee smaller countries. In September 1941, he wrote:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>In the present complete world confusion, it is not thought advisable at this time to reconstitute a League of Nations which, because of its size, makes for disagreement and inaction... There seem no reason why the principle of trusteeship in private affairs should be not be extended to the international field. Trusteeship is based on the principle of unselfish service. For a time at least there are many minor children among the peoples of the world who need trustees in their relations with other nations and people, just as there are many adult nations or peoples which must be led back into a spirit of good conduct.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGaddis197224_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaddis197224-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>The State Department had begun drafting a postwar successor to the League of Nations under the auspices of Roosevelt while the United States was still formally a neutral power.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBosco200914_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBosco200914-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Roosevelt was reluctant to publicly announce his plans for creating a postwar international body. He was aware of the risk that the American public might reject his proposals, and he did not want to repeat <a href="/wiki/Woodrow_Wilson" title="Woodrow Wilson">Woodrow Wilson</a>'s struggle to convince the Senate to approve American membership in the League of Nations. When the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Charter" title="Atlantic Charter">Atlantic Charter</a> was issued in August 1941, Roosevelt had ensured that the charter omitted mentioning any American commitment towards the establishment of a new international body after the war.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGaddis197225–26_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaddis197225–26-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Attack_on_Pearl_Harbor" title="Attack on Pearl Harbor">attack on Pearl Harbor</a> in December 1941 led to a change in Roosevelt's position. He transformed his trusteeship proposal into an organization centered around the Four Policemen: the United States, China, the Soviet Union, and Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGaddis197224_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaddis197224-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Plans_for_the_Four_Policemen">Plans for the Four Policemen</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Four_Policemen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Plans for the Four Policemen">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:United_Nations_organization_sketch_by_Franklin_Roosevelt_with_the_Four_Policemen_in_1943.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/United_Nations_organization_sketch_by_Franklin_Roosevelt_with_the_Four_Policemen_in_1943.jpg/220px-United_Nations_organization_sketch_by_Franklin_Roosevelt_with_the_Four_Policemen_in_1943.jpg" width="220" height="253" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/United_Nations_organization_sketch_by_Franklin_Roosevelt_with_the_Four_Policemen_in_1943.jpg/330px-United_Nations_organization_sketch_by_Franklin_Roosevelt_with_the_Four_Policemen_in_1943.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/United_Nations_organization_sketch_by_Franklin_Roosevelt_with_the_Four_Policemen_in_1943.jpg/440px-United_Nations_organization_sketch_by_Franklin_Roosevelt_with_the_Four_Policemen_in_1943.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1440" data-file-height="1659" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:United_Nations_organization_sketch_by_Franklin_Roosevelt_with_the_Four_Policemen_in_1943.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1943 sketch by Franklin Roosevelt of the United Nations' original three branches. The branch on the right represents the Four Policemen.</div></div></div>
<p>The idea that great powers should "police" the world had been discussed by President Roosevelt as early as August 1941, during his first meeting with Winston Churchill. Roosevelt made his first references to the Four Policemen proposal in early 1942.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKimball199185_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKimball199185-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> He presented his postwar plans to Molotov,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDallek1995342_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDallek1995342-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> who had arrived in Washington on May 29 to discuss the possibility of launching a second front in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGaddis197268_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaddis197268-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Roosevelt told Molotov that the Big Four must unite after the war to police the world and disarm aggressor states.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKimball199185_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKimball199185-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> When Molotov asked about the role of other countries, Roosevelt answered by opining that too many "policemen" could lead to infighting, but he was open to the idea of allowing other allied countries to participate.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKimball199185_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKimball199185-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> A memorandum of the conference summarizes their conversation:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The President told Molotov that he visualized the enforced disarmament of our enemies and, indeed, some of our friends after the war; that he thought that the United States, England, Russia and perhaps China should police the world and enforce disarmament by inspection. The President said that he visualized Germany, Italy, Japan, France, Czechoslovakia, Rumania and other nations would not be permitted to have military forces. He stated that other nations might join the first four mentioned after experience proved they could be trusted.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUnited_States_Department_of_State1942573_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUnited_States_Department_of_State1942573-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>Roosevelt and Molotov continued their discussion of the Four Policemen in a second meeting on June 1. Molotov informed the President that Stalin was willing to support Roosevelt's plans for maintaining postwar peace through the Four Policemen and enforced disarmament. Roosevelt also raised the issue of postwar decolonization. He suggested that former colonies should undergo a period of transition under the governance of an international trusteeship prior to their independence.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDallek1995342_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDallek1995342-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUnited_States_Department_of_State1942580_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUnited_States_Department_of_State1942580-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>China was brought in as a member of the Big Four and a future member of the Four Policemen. Roosevelt was in favor of recognizing China as a great power because he was certain that the Chinese would side with the Americans against the Soviets. He said to British Foreign Secretary <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Eden" title="Anthony Eden">Anthony Eden</a>, "In any serious conflict of policy with Russia, [China] would undoubtedly line up on our side." The President believed that a pro-American China would be useful for the United States should the Americans, Soviets, and Chinese agree to jointly occupy Japan and Korea after the war.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDallek1995390_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDallek1995390-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> When Molotov voiced concerns about the stability of China, Roosevelt responded by saying that the combined "population of our nations and friends was well over a billion people."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUnited_States_Department_of_State1942573_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUnited_States_Department_of_State1942573-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDallek1995342_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDallek1995342-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Churchill objected to Roosevelt's inclusion of China as one of the Big Four because he feared that the Americans were trying to undermine Britain's colonial holdings in Asia. In October 1942, Churchill told Eden that Republican China represented a "<a href="/wiki/Faggot_voter" title="Faggot voter">faggot vote</a> on the side of the United States in any attempt to liquidate the British overseas empire."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDallek1995389_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDallek1995389-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Eden shared this view with Churchill and expressed skepticism that China, which was then in the midst of a civil war, could ever return to a stable nation. Roosevelt responded to Churchill's criticism by telling Eden that "China might become a very useful power in the Far East to help police Japan" and that he was fully supportive of offering more aid to China.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDallek1995390_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDallek1995390-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Roosevelt's Four Policemen proposal received criticism from the liberal internationalists, who wanted power to be more evenly distributed among the member nations of the UN. Internationalists were concerned that the Four Policemen could lead to a new Quadruple Alliance.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGaddis197227_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaddis197227-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation_of_the_United_Nations">Formation of the United Nations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Four_Policemen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Formation of the United Nations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On New Year's Day 1942, the representatives of Allied "Big Four", the United States, the United Kingdom, the Soviet Union and China, signed a short document which later came to be known as the <a href="/wiki/Declaration_by_United_Nations" title="Declaration by United Nations">Declaration by United Nations</a> and the next day the representatives of twenty-two other nations added their signatures.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUnited_Nations_Official_Website_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUnited_Nations_Official_Website-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMa2003203–204_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMa2003203–204-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
A new plan for the United Nations was drafted by the State Department in April 1944. It kept the emphasis on <a href="/wiki/Great_power" title="Great power">great power</a> solidarity that was central to Roosevelt's Four Policemen proposal for the United Nations. The members of the Big Four would serve as permanent members of the United Nation's Security Council. Each of the four permanent members would be given a <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council_veto_power" title="United Nations Security Council veto power">United Nations Security Council veto power</a>, which would override any UN resolution that went against the interests of one of the Big Four. However, the State Department had compromised with the liberal internationalists. Membership eligibility was widened to include all nation states fighting against the Axis powers instead of a select few. The <a href="/wiki/Dumbarton_Oaks_Conference" title="Dumbarton Oaks Conference">Dumbarton Oaks Conference</a> convened in August 1944 to discuss plans for the postwar United Nations with delegations from the United States, United Kingdom, Soviet Union, and China.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGaddis197227_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaddis197227-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The Big Four were the only four sponsoring countries of the <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco_Conference" class="mw-redirect" title="San Francisco Conference">San Francisco Conference</a> of 1945 and their heads of the delegations took turns as chairman of the plenary meetings.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUnited_Nations_Official_Website1945_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUnited_Nations_Official_Website1945-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> During this conference, the Big Four and their allies signed the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Charter" title="United Nations Charter">United Nations Charter</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGaddis197228_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaddis197228-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Four_Policemen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In the words of a former Undersecretary General of the UN, Sir <a href="/wiki/Brian_Urquhart" title="Brian Urquhart">Brian Urquhart</a>:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>It was a pragmatic system based on the primacy of the strong — a "<a href="/wiki/Trustee" title="Trustee">trusteeship</a> of the powerful," as he then called it, or, as he put it later, "the Four Policemen." The concept was, as [Senator Arthur H.] <a href="/wiki/Arthur_H._Vandenberg" class="mw-redirect" title="Arthur H. Vandenberg">Vandenberg</a> noted in his diary in April 1944, "anything but a wild-eyed internationalist dream of a world state.... It is based virtually on a four-power alliance." Eventually this proved to be both the potential strength and the actual weakness of the future UN, an organization theoretically based on a concert of great powers whose own mutual hostility, as it turned out, was itself the greatest potential threat to world peace.<sup id="cite_ref-sheriff_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sheriff-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Four_Policemen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">Allies of World War II</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Global_policeman" title="Global policeman">Global policeman</a></li></ul>
